</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Literary genre</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the artistic genre. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Fantasy (disambiguation)">Fantasy (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Fantasy fiction" redirects here. For the magazine, see <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_Fiction" title="Fantasy Fiction">Fantasy Fiction</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<span class="nowrap"><span class="skin-invert-image noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Dragon-149393.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dragon-149393.svg/20px-Dragon-149393.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dragon-149393.svg/40px-Dragon-149393.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="529" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Speculative_fiction" title="Portal:Speculative fiction">Portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239400231" /><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Speculative_fiction_sidebar" title="Template:Speculative fiction sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Speculative_fiction_sidebar" title="Template talk:Speculative fiction sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Speculative_fiction_sidebar" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Speculative fiction sidebar"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Fantasy</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> of <a href="/wiki/Speculative_fiction" title="Speculative fiction">speculative fiction</a> that involves <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magic_(supernatural)" title="Magic (supernatural)">magical</a> elements, often including completely <a href="/wiki/Fictional_universe" title="Fictional universe">imaginary realms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legendary_creature" title="Legendary creature">creatures</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The genre's roots lie in <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_literature" title="Fantasy literature">fantasy literature</a> and drama. From the twentieth century onward, it has expanded into various media, including film, television, <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animation" title="Animation">animation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Video_games" class="mw-redirect" title="Video games">video games</a>.
</p><p>The expression <i>fantastic literature</i> is often used for this genre by Anglophone literary critics.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An archaic spelling for the term is <i>phantasy</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fantasy is generally distinguished from the genres of <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">horror</a> by an absence of scientific or macabre themes, although these can occur in fantasy. In <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>, the fantasy genre predominantly features settings that reflect the actual Earth, but with some sense of otherness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Characteristics">Characteristics</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Li_Song-Skeleton_Fantasy_Show.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Li_Song-Skeleton_Fantasy_Show.jpg/250px-Li_Song-Skeleton_Fantasy_Show.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Li_Song-Skeleton_Fantasy_Show.jpg/330px-Li_Song-Skeleton_Fantasy_Show.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Li_Song-Skeleton_Fantasy_Show.jpg/500px-Li_Song-Skeleton_Fantasy_Show.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1980" data-file-height="2016" /></a><figcaption>Painting <i>Skeleton Fantasy Show</i> (骷髏幻戲圖) by <a href="/wiki/Li_Song_(painter)" title="Li Song (painter)">Li Song</a> (1190–1264)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many works of fantasy use <a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> elements as a main <a href="/wiki/Plot_(narrative)" title="Plot (narrative)">plot</a> element, <a href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theme (literature)">theme</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Setting_(fiction)" class="mw-redirect" title="Setting (fiction)">setting</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Magic, magic practitioners (<a href="/wiki/Sorcerer_(fantasy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sorcerer (fantasy)">sorcerers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Witch" class="mw-redirect" title="Witch">witches</a> and so on) and <a href="/wiki/Legendary_creature" title="Legendary creature">magical creatures</a> are common in many of these worlds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An identifying trait of fantasy is the author's use of narrative elements that do not have to rely on history or nature to be coherent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This differs from realistic fiction in that realistic fiction has to attend to the history and natural laws of reality, where fantasy does not. In writing fantasy the author uses <a href="/wiki/Worldbuilding" title="Worldbuilding">worldbuilding</a> to create characters, situations, and settings that may not be possible in reality.
</p><p>Many fantasy authors use real-world folklore and mythology as inspiration;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and although another defining characteristic of the fantasy genre is the inclusion of supernatural elements, such as magic,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> this does not have to be the case.
</p><p>Fantasy has often been compared to <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">horror</a> because they are the major categories of <a href="/wiki/Speculative_fiction" title="Speculative fiction">speculative fiction</a>. Fantasy is distinguished from science fiction by the plausibility of the narrative elements. A science fiction narrative is unlikely, though seemingly possible through logical scientific or technological extrapolation, where fantasy narratives do not need to be scientifically possible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Authors have to rely on the readers' <a href="/wiki/Suspension_of_disbelief" title="Suspension of disbelief">suspension of disbelief</a>, an acceptance of the unbelievable or impossible for the sake of enjoyment, in order to write effective fantasies. Despite both genres' heavy reliance on the supernatural, fantasy and horror are distinguishable from one another. Horror primarily evokes fear through the protagonists' weaknesses or inability to deal with the antagonists.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_fantasy" title="History of fantasy">History of fantasy</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Early_history">Early history</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_history_of_fantasy" title="Early history of fantasy">Early history of fantasy</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_violet_fairy_book_(1906)_(14730388126).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730388126%29.jpg/250px-The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730388126%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="154" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730388126%29.jpg/500px-The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730388126%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2768" data-file-height="1708" /></a><figcaption>Illustration from the story <i>"</i><a href="/wiki/The_Fairy_Aurora" title="The Fairy Aurora">The Fairy of the Dawn</a>" in <i><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lang%27s_Fairy_Books#The_Violet_Fairy_Book_(1901)" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew Lang&#39;s Fairy Books">The Violet Fairy Book</a></i> (1906)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_violet_fairy_book_(1906)_(14730393436).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730393436%29.jpg/250px-The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730393436%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="403" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730393436%29.jpg/500px-The_violet_fairy_book_%281906%29_%2814730393436%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1678" data-file-height="2702" /></a><figcaption>Another illustration from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Violet_Fairy_Book" class="mw-redirect" title="The Violet Fairy Book">The Violet Fairy Book</a></i> (1906)</figcaption></figure>
<p>While elements of the supernatural and the fantastic were part of literature from its beginning, fantasy elements also occur throughout ancient religious texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ancient Babylonian creation epic, the <i><a href="/wiki/En%C3%BBma_Eli%C5%A1" class="mw-redirect" title="Enûma Eliš">Enûma Eliš</a></i>, in which the god <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a> slays the goddess <a href="/wiki/Tiamat" title="Tiamat">Tiamat</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Keefer2008&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keefer2008-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> reflects the theme of cosmic conflict between good and evil, which is characteristic of the modern fantasy genre.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Keefer2008&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keefer2008-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Genres of romantic and fantasy literature also existed in ancient Egypt.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moscati&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moscati-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>The Tales of the Court of King Khufu</i>, which is preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Westcar_Papyrus" title="Westcar Papyrus">Westcar Papyrus</a> and was probably written in the middle of the second half of the eighteenth century BC, preserves a mixture of stories with elements of historical fiction, fantasy, and satire.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Egyptian funerary texts preserve mythological tales,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moscati&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moscati-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the most significant of which are the <a href="/wiki/Osiris_myth" title="Osiris myth">myths of Osiris</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Horus" title="Horus">Horus</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moscati&#95;16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moscati-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Myth with fantastic elements intended for adults were a major genre of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">ancient Greek literature</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The comedies of <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a> are filled with fantastic elements,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> particularly his play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birds_(play)" title="The Birds (play)">The Birds</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in which an Athenian man persuades the world's birds to build <a href="/wiki/Cloudcuckooland" class="mw-redirect" title="Cloudcuckooland">a city in the clouds</a> and thereby challenges <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>'s authority.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Apuleius" title="Apuleius">Apuleius</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Ass" title="The Golden Ass">The Golden Ass</a></i> are both works that influenced the development of the fantasy genre<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> by taking mythic elements and weaving them into personal accounts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Both works involve complex narratives in which humans beings are transformed into animals or inanimate objects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonic</a> teachings and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christian_theology#Early_Christian_theology" title="History of Christian theology">early Christian theology</a> are major influences on the modern fantasy genre.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> used <a href="/wiki/Allegories" class="mw-redirect" title="Allegories">allegories</a> to convey many of his teachings,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and early Christian writers interpreted both the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testaments</a> as employing <a href="/wiki/Parable" title="Parable">parables</a> to convey spiritual truths.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This ability to find meaning in a story that is not literally true became the foundation for developing the modern fantasy genre.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mathews2002&#95;20-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathews2002-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Islamic, Hindu, and Chinese sources contain fantasy elements as well. The best-known fiction from the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a> (The Arabian Nights)</i>, which is a compilation of ancient and medieval folk tales. Various characters from this epic have become cultural icons in Western culture, such as <a href="/wiki/Aladdin" title="Aladdin">Aladdin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinbad" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinbad">Sinbad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ali_Baba" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali Baba">Ali Baba</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chinoiserie&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chinoiserie-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a> was an evolution of the earlier <a href="/wiki/Vedic_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Vedic mythology">Vedic mythology</a> and had many more fantastical stories and characters, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_epic_poetry" title="Indian epic poetry">Indian epics</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Panchatantra" title="Panchatantra">Panchatantra</a></i> (<i>Fables of Bidpai</i>), for example, used animal <a href="/wiki/Fable" title="Fable">fables</a> and magical tales to illustrate the central Indian principles of <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a>. Chinese traditions have been particularly influential in the vein of fantasy known as <a href="/wiki/Chinoiserie" title="Chinoiserie">Chinoiserie</a>, which includes such writers as <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bramah" title="Ernest Bramah">Ernest Bramah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barry_Hughart" title="Barry Hughart">Barry Hughart</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chinoiserie&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chinoiserie-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i> is among the best known of the Old English tales in the English-speaking world, and it has deeply influenced the fantasy genre; several fantasy works have retold the story, for example, <a href="/wiki/John_Champlin_Gardner_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="John Champlin Gardner Jr.">
John Gardner</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Grendel_(novel)" title="Grendel (novel)">Grendel</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, as found in the <a href="/wiki/Elder_Edda" class="mw-redirect" title="Elder Edda">Elder Edda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Younger_Edda" class="mw-redirect" title="Younger Edda">Younger Edda</a> collections, includes such figures as the god <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a> and his fellow <a href="/wiki/Aesir" class="mw-redirect" title="Aesir">Aesir</a>, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Norse_dwarf" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse dwarf">dwarves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elf" title="Elf">elves</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_dragon" title="European dragon">dragons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6tunn" title="Jötunn">giants</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These elements have been directly imported into various fantasy works. The distinct folklores of Ireland, Wales, and Scotland have sometimes been used indiscriminately for "Celtic" fantasy, sometimes with great success; other writers have specified the use of a single source.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-celtic&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-celtic-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Welsh</a> tradition has been particularly influential, because of its connection to the legendary <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> and its collection into a single work, the epic <a href="/wiki/Mabinogion" title="Mabinogion">Mabinogion</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-celtic&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-celtic-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are many works where the boundary between fantasy and other genres is unclear: did the writers believe in the possibility of the marvels in the play <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night&#39;s Dream">A Midsummer Night's Dream</a></i> or the romance in <i><a href="/wiki/Sir_Gawain_and_the_Green_Knight" title="Sir Gawain and the Green Knight">Sir Gawain and the Green Knight</a></i>? This question makes it difficult to distinguish when fantasy began, in its modern sense.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Modern_fantasy">Modern fantasy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern fantasy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Curdie_went_on_after_her,_flashing_his_torch_about..jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Curdie_went_on_after_her%2C_flashing_his_torch_about..jpg/250px-Curdie_went_on_after_her%2C_flashing_his_torch_about..jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="347" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Curdie_went_on_after_her%2C_flashing_his_torch_about..jpg/429px-Curdie_went_on_after_her%2C_flashing_his_torch_about..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Curdie_went_on_after_her%2C_flashing_his_torch_about..jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="596" /></a><figcaption>Illustration from 1920 edition of <a href="/wiki/George_MacDonald" title="George MacDonald">George MacDonald</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Princess_and_the_Goblin" title="The Princess and the Goblin">The Princess and the Goblin</a></i></figcaption></figure>
<p>Although pre-dated by <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>'s story <i><a href="/wiki/The_King_of_the_Golden_River" title="The King of the Golden River">The King of the Golden River</a></i> (1841), the history of modern fantasy literature is usually said to begin with <a href="/wiki/George_MacDonald" title="George MacDonald">George MacDonald</a>, the Scottish author of such novels as <i><a href="/wiki/Phantastes" title="Phantastes">Phantastes</a></i> (1858) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Princess_and_the_Goblin" title="The Princess and the Goblin">The Princess and the Goblin</a></i> (1872); the former is widely considered to be the first fantasy novel ever written for adults. MacDonald was a major influence on both <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a> and <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The other major fantasy author of this era was <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a>, an English poet who wrote several novels in the latter part of the century, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wood_Beyond_the_World" title="The Wood Beyond the World">The Wood Beyond the World</a></i> (1894) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Well_at_the_World%27s_End" title="The Well at the World&#39;s End">The Well at the World's End</a></i> (1896).
</p><p>Despite MacDonald's future influence with the novel <i><a href="/wiki/At_the_Back_of_the_North_Wind" title="At the Back of the North Wind">At the Back of the North Wind</a></i> (1871), Morris's popularity with his contemporaries, and <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wonderful_Visit" title="The Wonderful Visit">The Wonderful Visit</a></i> (1895), it was not until the 20th century that fantasy fiction began to reach a large audience. <a href="/wiki/Lord_Dunsany" title="Lord Dunsany">Lord Dunsany</a> established the genre's popularity in both the novel and the short story forms. <a href="/wiki/H._Rider_Haggard" title="H. Rider Haggard">H. Rider Haggard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Rice_Burroughs" title="Edgar Rice Burroughs">Edgar Rice Burroughs</a> began to write fantasy around this time. These authors, along with <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Merritt" class="mw-redirect" title="Abraham Merritt">Abraham Merritt</a>, established what was known as the <i>lost world</i> subgenre; this was the most popular form of fantasy in the early decades of the 20th century, although several classic children's fantasies, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan_(play_and_novel)" title="Peter Pan (play and novel)">Peter Pan</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz" title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz">The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</a></i>, were also published around this time.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Juvenile_fantasy" class="mw-redirect" title="Juvenile fantasy">Juvenile</a> fantasy was considered more acceptable than fantasy intended for adults, with the consequence that writers who wished to write fantasy for adults needed to fit their work into forms aimed at children.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a> wrote fantasy in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Wonder-Book_for_Girls_and_Boys" title="A Wonder-Book for Girls and Boys">A Wonder-Book for Girls and Boys</a></i>, intended for children,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although his works for adults only verged on fantasy. For many years, this book and successes such as the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland" title="Alice&#39;s Adventures in Wonderland">Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</a></i> (1865) created a circular effect: all fantasy works, even the later series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings" title="The Lord of the Rings">The Lord of the Rings</a></i>, were therefore classified as children's literature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Political and social trends can affect a society's reception of fantasy. In the early 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New Culture Movement</a>'s enthusiasm for Westernization and science in China compelled them to condemn the fantastical <i><a href="/wiki/Shenmo" class="mw-redirect" title="Shenmo">shenmo</a></i> genre of traditional Chinese literature. The spells and magical creatures in these novels were viewed as superstitious and backward, products of a feudal society hindering the modernization of China. Stories of the supernatural continued to be denounced once the Communists rose to power, and mainland China experienced a revival in fantasy only after the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> had ended.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ch20th&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ch20th-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fantasy became a genre of <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">pulp magazines</a> published in the West. The first all-fantasy fiction magazine, <i><a href="/wiki/Weird_Tales" title="Weird Tales">Weird Tales</a></i>, was published in 1923. Many similar magazines eventually followed, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Magazine_of_Fantasy_and_Science_Fiction" class="mw-redirect" title="The Magazine of Fantasy and Science Fiction">The Magazine of Fantasy and Science Fiction</a></i> (<i>F&amp;SF</i>). When this magazine was founded in 1949, the pulp format was at the height of its popularity; <i>F&amp;SF</i> was instrumental in bringing fantasy fiction to a wide audience in both the US and the UK. Such magazines were also instrumental in the rise of science fiction, and the two genres were first associated with each other around this time.
</p><p>By 1950, <a href="/wiki/Sword_and_sorcery" title="Sword and sorcery">sword and sorcery</a> fiction had begun to find a wider audience, with the success of <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Howard" title="Robert E. Howard">Robert E. Howard</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Conan_the_Barbarian" title="Conan the Barbarian">Conan the Barbarian</a> stories and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Leiber" title="Fritz Leiber">Fritz Leiber</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fafhrd_and_the_Gray_Mouser" title="Fafhrd and the Gray Mouser">Fafhrd and the Gray Mouser</a> stories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, it was the advent of <a href="/wiki/High_fantasy" title="High fantasy">high fantasy</a>—especially J. R. R. Tolkien's novels <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit">The Hobbit</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings" title="The Lord of the Rings">The Lord of the Rings</a></i>, which reached new heights of popularity in the late 1960s—that allowed fantasy to <a href="/wiki/Tolkien%27s_impact_on_fantasy" title="Tolkien&#39;s impact on fantasy">enter the mainstream</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Yolen&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yolen-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several other series, such as C. S. Lewis's <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicles_of_Narnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronicles of Narnia">The Chronicles of Narnia</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Earthsea" title="Earthsea">Earthsea</a></i>, helped to cement the genre's popularity.
</p><p>The popularity of the fantasy genre has continued to increase in the 21st century, as evidenced by the best-selling status of several series: <a href="/wiki/J._K._Rowling" title="J. K. Rowling">J. K. Rowling</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter" title="Harry Potter">Harry Potter</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Jordan" title="Robert Jordan">Robert Jordan</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wheel_of_Time" title="The Wheel of Time">The Wheel of Time</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/George_R._R._Martin" title="George R. R. Martin">George R. R. Martin</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Song_of_Ice_and_Fire" title="A Song of Ice and Fire">Song of Ice and Fire</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Erikson" title="Steven Erikson">Steven Erikson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Malazan_Book_of_the_Fallen" title="Malazan Book of the Fallen">Malazan Book of the Fallen</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Sanderson" title="Brandon Sanderson">Brandon Sanderson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stormlight_Archive" title="The Stormlight Archive">The Stormlight Archive</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mistborn" title="Mistborn">Mistborn</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/A._Sapkowski" class="mw-redirect" title="A. Sapkowski">A. Sapkowski</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Witcher" title="The Witcher">The Witcher</a></i>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Media">Media</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Media"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Fantasy art">Fantasy art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_film" title="Fantasy film">Fantasy film</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_television" title="Fantasy television">Fantasy television</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_game" title="Role-playing game">Role-playing game</a></div>
<p>Several fantasy film adaptations have achieved blockbuster status, most notably <a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings_(film_series)" title="The Lord of the Rings (film series)"><i>The Lord of the Rings</i></a> film trilogy directed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jackson" title="Peter Jackson">Peter Jackson</a>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_(film_series)" title="Harry Potter (film series)">Harry Potter</a></i> films, two of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_films#Highest-grossing_franchises_and_film_series" title="List of highest-grossing films">highest-grossing film series</a> in cinema history.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:RPG-2009-Berlin-2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/RPG-2009-Berlin-2.jpg/250px-RPG-2009-Berlin-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="187" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/RPG-2009-Berlin-2.jpg/500px-RPG-2009-Berlin-2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="749" /></a><figcaption>A group playing a tabletop role-playing game (RPG). The Games Master is at left using a cardboard <a href="/wiki/Gamemaster%27s_screen" title="Gamemaster&#39;s screen">screen</a> to hide dice rolls from the players.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Fantasy <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_game" title="Role-playing game">role-playing games</a> (RPGs) span several media. <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons">Dungeons &amp; Dragons</a></i> (<i>D&amp;D</i>) was the first <a href="/wiki/Tabletop_role-playing_game" title="Tabletop role-playing game">tabletop role-playing game</a>, and it remains the most successful and influential. According to a 1999 survey in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, six percent of 12- to 35-year-olds have played role-playing games. Of those who play regularly, two thirds play <i>D&amp;D</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Products branded <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> accounted for over fifty percent of the RPG products sold in 2005.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Science_fantasy" title="Science fantasy">science fantasy</a> role-playing game series <i><a href="/wiki/Final_Fantasy" title="Final Fantasy">Final Fantasy</a></i> has been an icon of the <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_video_game" title="Role-playing video game">role-playing video game</a> genre. (As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, it was still among the top ten <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_video_game_franchises" title="List of best-selling video game franchises">best-selling video game franchises</a>.) The first <a href="/wiki/Collectible_card_game" title="Collectible card game">collectible card game</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Magic:_The_Gathering" title="Magic: The Gathering">Magic: The Gathering</a></i>, has a fantasy theme and is similarly dominant in the industry.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Classification">Classification</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="By_theme_(subgenres)"><span id="By_theme_.28subgenres.29"></span>By theme (subgenres)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: By theme (subgenres)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1305433154">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .ambox{margin:0 10%}}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .ambox{display:none!important}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced&#95;section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/60px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/120px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Fantasy" title="Special:EditPage/Fantasy">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2024</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_genres#Fantasy" title="List of genres">List of genres §&#160;Fantasy</a></div>
<p>Fantasy encompasses numerous <a href="/wiki/Subgenre" class="mw-redirect" title="Subgenre">subgenres</a> characterized by particular themes or settings, or by an overlap with other literary genres or forms of speculative fiction. These subgenres include the following:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1184024115">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 20em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bangsian_fantasy" title="Bangsian fantasy">Bangsian fantasy</a>, interactions with famous historical figures in the afterlife, named for <a href="/wiki/John_Kendrick_Bangs" title="John Kendrick Bangs">John Kendrick Bangs</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Comic_fantasy" class="mw-redirect" title="Comic fantasy">Comic fantasy</a>, humorous in tone</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Contemporary_fantasy" title="Contemporary fantasy">Contemporary fantasy</a>, set in the modern world or a world based on a <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_history" title="Contemporary history">contemporary era</a>, but involving magic or other supernatural elements</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dark_fantasy" title="Dark fantasy">Dark fantasy</a>, including elements of <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">horror fiction</a></li>
<li>Extruded fantasy product, derogatory term for derivative works<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fable" title="Fable">Fables</a>, stories with non-human characters, leading to morals or lessons</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fairy_tale" title="Fairy tale">Fairy tales</a> themselves, as well as <a href="/wiki/Fairytale_fantasy" title="Fairytale fantasy">fairytale fantasy</a>, which draws on fairy tale themes</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Fantastic poetry">Fantastic poetry</a>, poetry with a fantastic theme</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Fantastique" title="Fantastique">Fantastique</a></i>, a genre characterized by the intrusion of supernatural elements into the realistic framework of a story, accompanied by uncertainty about their existence</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fantasy_of_manners" title="Fantasy of manners">Fantasy of manners</a>, or mannerpunk, focusing on matters of social standing in the style of a <a href="/wiki/Comedy_of_manners" title="Comedy of manners">comedy of manners</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gaslamp_fantasy" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaslamp fantasy">Gaslamp fantasy</a>, using a <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian</a> setting, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Gothic_fiction" title="Gothic fiction">gothic fiction</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gods_and_demons_fiction" title="Gods and demons fiction">Gods and demons fiction</a> (<i>shenmo</i>), involving the gods and monsters of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">Chinese mythology</a></li>
<li><em><a href="/wiki/Grimdark" title="Grimdark">Grimdark</a></em> fiction, a somewhat tongue-in-cheek label for fiction with an especially violent tone or <a href="/wiki/Dystopia" title="Dystopia">dystopian</a> themes</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hard_fantasy" title="Hard fantasy">Hard fantasy</a>, whose supernatural aspects are intended to be internally consistent and explainable, named by analogy to <a href="/wiki/Hard_science_fiction" title="Hard science fiction">hard science fiction</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Heroic_fantasy" class="mw-redirect" title="Heroic fantasy">Heroic fantasy</a>, concerned with stories of heroes in imaginary lands</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/High_fantasy" title="High fantasy">High fantasy</a> or epic fantasy, characterized by a plot and themes of epic scale, often set in an alternate world.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Historical_fantasy" title="Historical fantasy">Historical fantasy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historical_fiction" title="Historical fiction">historical fiction</a> with fantasy elements</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isekai" title="Isekai">Isekai</a>, people transported from the real world to a different one, mainly in Japanese fiction (<a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Light_novel" title="Light novel">light novels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Juvenile_fantasy" class="mw-redirect" title="Juvenile fantasy">Juvenile fantasy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_literature" title="Children&#39;s literature">children's literature</a> with fantasy elements</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/LitRPG" title="LitRPG">LitRPG</a>, set in a table-top or computer role-playing game, and depicting the progression and mechanics of the game</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Low_fantasy" title="Low fantasy">Low fantasy</a>, characterized by few or non-intrusive supernatural elements, often in contrast to high fantasy</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Magic_realism" class="mw-redirect" title="Magic realism">Magic realism</a>, also a genre of <a href="/wiki/Literary_fiction" title="Literary fiction">literary fiction</a>, is set in the real world where magic or the supernatural is considered normal or insignificant.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Magical_girl" title="Magical girl">Magical girl</a> fantasy, involving young girls with magical powers, mainly in Japanese fiction</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paranormal_romance" title="Paranormal romance">Paranormal romance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romance_novel" title="Romance novel">romantic fiction</a> with <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> or <a href="/wiki/Legendary_creature" title="Legendary creature">fantastic</a> creatures</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Romantic_fantasy" title="Romantic fantasy">Romantic fantasy</a>, focusing on romantic relationships</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Science_fantasy" title="Science fantasy">Science fantasy</a>, fantasy incorporating elements from <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> such as advanced technology, aliens and space travel, but also fantastical things</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Steampunk" title="Steampunk">Steampunk</a>, a genre which is sometimes a kind of fantasy, with elements from 19th century steam technology (historical fantasy and science fantasy both overlap with this genre)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sword_and_sorcery" title="Sword and sorcery">Sword and sorcery</a>, adventures of sword-wielding heroes, generally more limited in scope than epic fantasy</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Urban_fantasy" title="Urban fantasy">Urban fantasy</a>, set in a city in the real world.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Weird_fiction" title="Weird fiction">Weird fiction</a>, macabre and unsettling stories from before the terms <em>fantasy</em> and <em>horror</em> were widely used; see also the more modern forms of <a href="/wiki/Slipstream_(genre)" class="mw-redirect" title="Slipstream (genre)">slipstream fiction</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Weird" class="mw-redirect" title="New Weird">New Weird</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xianxia_(genre)" class="mw-redirect" title="Xianxia (genre)">Xianxia</a>, Chinese martial-arts fiction often incorporating fantasy elements, such as gods, fairies, demons, magical realms, and reincarnation</li></ul>
</div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="By_narrative_function">By narrative function</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: By narrative function"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In her book <i><a href="/wiki/Rhetorics_of_Fantasy" title="Rhetorics of Fantasy">Rhetorics of Fantasy</a></i> (2008),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Farah_Mendlesohn" title="Farah Mendlesohn">Farah Mendlesohn</a> proposes a <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(general)" class="mw-redirect" title="Taxonomy (general)">taxonomy</a> of fantasy, as "determined by the means by which the fantastic enters the narrated world."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (She notes some fantasies fit none of the patterns in this taxonomy.) The taxonomy categories are as follows: 
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1228772891">.mw-parser-output .glossary dt{margin-top:0.4em}.mw-parser-output .glossary dt+dt{margin-top:-0.2em}.mw-parser-output .glossary .templatequote{margin-top:0;margin-bottom:-0.5em}</style>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt id="portal&#95;fantasy"><dfn><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1238216509">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#0f4dc9}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#0f4dc9}}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="Portal&#95;fantasy"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Portal fantasy</span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd>In <em>portal-quest fantasy</em> or <em>portal fantasy</em>, a <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_world" title="Fantasy world">fantasy world</a> is entered, within which the fantastic elements remain contained. A portal-quest fantasy typically tends to be a <a href="/wiki/Quest" title="Quest">quest</a>-type narrative, whose main challenge is navigating the fantastical world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Notable examples include <a href="/wiki/L._Frank_Baum" title="L. Frank Baum">L. Frank Baum</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz" title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz">The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</a></i> (1900), <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>' novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion,_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</a></i> (1950),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_R._Donaldson" title="Stephen R. Donaldson">Stephen R. Donaldson</a>'s series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronicles_of_Thomas_Covenant" title="The Chronicles of Thomas Covenant">The Chronicles of Thomas Covenant</a></i> (late 1970s).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Senft2020&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Senft2020-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Japan, the genre of portal fantasy is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Isekai" title="Isekai">isekai</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">異世界</span>; <abbr title="translation">transl.</abbr> "different world" or "otherworld")</span>, which has developed its own set of conventions.</dd>

<dt id="immersive&#95;fantasy"><dfn><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238216509" /><span class="vanchor"><span id="Immersive&#95;fantasy"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Immersive fantasy</span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd>In <em>immersive fantasy</em>, the <a href="/wiki/Secondary_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary world">fictional world</a> is seen as complete; its fantastic elements remain unquestioned within the context of the story; and the reader perceives the world through the eyes and ears of characters native to the setting. This narrative mode "consciously negates the <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_wonder" title="Sense of wonder">sense of wonder</a>" often associated with <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>, according to Mendlesohn. She adds that "a sufficiently effective immersive fantasy may be indistinguishable from science fiction" as the fantastic "acquires a scientific cohesion all of its own". This similarity has led to disputes about how to classify novels such as <a href="/wiki/Mary_Gentle" title="Mary Gentle">Mary Gentle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ash:_A_Secret_History" title="Ash: A Secret History">Ash</a></i> (2000) and <a href="/wiki/China_Mi%C3%A9ville" title="China Miéville">China Miéville</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Perdido_Street_Station" title="Perdido Street Station">Perdido Street Station</a></i> (2000).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></dd>

<dt id="intrusion&#95;fantasy"><dfn><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238216509" /><span class="vanchor"><span id="Intrusion&#95;fantasy"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Intrusion fantasy</span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd>In <em>intrusion fantasy</em>, the fantastic intrudes on reality (as portal fantasies do not), and the protagonists' engagement with that intrusion drives the story. Usually <a href="/wiki/Literary_realism" title="Literary realism">realist</a> in style, these works assume the default world as their basis. Intrusion fantasies rely heavily on explanation and description.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Immersive and portal fantasies may themselves host intrusions. Classic intrusion fantasies include the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Dracula" title="Dracula">Dracula</a></i> (1897) by <a href="/wiki/Bram_Stoker" title="Bram Stoker">Bram Stoker</a> and the book <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Poppins_(book_series)" title="Mary Poppins (book series)">Mary Poppins</a></i> (1934) by <a href="/wiki/P._L._Travers" title="P. L. Travers">P. L. Travers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mendlesohn,&#95;Chapter&#95;3&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mendlesohn,_Chapter_3-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/French-speaking_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="French-speaking countries">French-speaking countries</a>, this genre is called <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastique" title="Fantastique">fantastique</a></i> and is considered to be distinct from fantasy.</dd>

<dt id="liminal&#95;fantasy"><dfn><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238216509" /><span class="vanchor"><span id="Liminal&#95;fantasy"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Liminal fantasy</span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd>In <em>liminal fantasy</em>, the fantastic enters a world that appears to be our own. The marvelous is perceived as normal by the protagonists, while it disconcerts and <a href="/wiki/Distancing_effect" title="Distancing effect">estranges</a> the reader. This is a relatively rare mode. Such fantasies often adopt an ironic, blasé tone, as opposed to the straight-faced <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">mimesis</a> more common in fantasy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Examples include <a href="/wiki/Joan_Aiken" title="Joan Aiken">Joan Aiken</a>'s stories about the Armitage family, who are amazed that unicorns appear on their lawn on a Tuesday, rather than a Monday.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mendlesohn,&#95;Chapter&#95;3&#95;43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mendlesohn,_Chapter_3-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Subculture">Subculture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Subculture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_fandom" title="Fantasy fandom">Fantasy fandom</a></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:NACollinsSeattle89A.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NACollinsSeattle89A.jpg/330px-NACollinsSeattle89A.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="125" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NACollinsSeattle89A.jpg/500px-NACollinsSeattle89A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NACollinsSeattle89A.jpg/960px-NACollinsSeattle89A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1378" data-file-height="572" /></a><figcaption>Fantasy writers on a panel at the World Fantasy Conference in Seattle, Washington, in 1989. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bryant" title="Edward Bryant">Ed Bryant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nancy_A._Collins" title="Nancy A. Collins">Nancy A. Collins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Edward_Wagner" title="Karl Edward Wagner">Karl Edward Wagner</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Publishers, editors, authors, artists, and scholars who are interested in the fantasy genre meet annually at the <a href="/wiki/World_Fantasy_Convention" title="World Fantasy Convention">World Fantasy Convention</a> (WFC). The <a href="/wiki/World_Fantasy_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="World Fantasy Awards">World Fantasy Awards</a> are presented at this convention. The first WFC was held in 1975, and it has been held annually since that time (in a different city each year).
</p><p>In addition, many science fiction conventions, such as Florida's <a href="/w/index.php?title=FX_Show&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="FX Show (page does not exist)">FX Show</a> and <a href="/wiki/MegaCon" title="MegaCon">MegaCon</a>, cater to fantasy and horror fans. Anime conventions, such as <a href="/wiki/Ohayocon" title="Ohayocon">Ohayocon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anime_Expo" title="Anime Expo">Anime Expo</a>, often feature showings of fantasy, science fantasy, and dark fantasy series and films; examples include <i><a href="/wiki/Majutsushi_Orphen" class="mw-redirect" title="Majutsushi Orphen">Majutsushi Orphen</a></i> (fantasy), <i><a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon" title="Sailor Moon">Sailor Moon</a></i> (urban fantasy), <i><a href="/wiki/Berserk_(manga)" title="Berserk (manga)">Berserk</a></i> (dark fantasy), and <i><a href="/wiki/Spirited_Away" title="Spirited Away">Spirited Away</a></i> (fantasy). Many science-fiction/fantasy and anime conventions also emphasize or cater to one or more of the subcultures within the main cultures:
</p>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Cosplay" title="Cosplay">cosplay</a> subculture, in which people make or wear costumes based on existing or self-created characters, sometimes acting out skits or plays as well</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Fan_fiction" title="Fan fiction">fan fiction</a> subculture</li>
<li>the fan video or <a href="/wiki/Anime_music_video" title="Anime music video">AMV</a> subculture</li>
<li>the large internet subculture, which is devoted to reading and writing prose fiction or <a href="/wiki/Doujinshi" title="Doujinshi">doujinshi</a> in or related to those genres</li></ul>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Avon_Fantasy_Reader_18.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Avon_Fantasy_Reader_18.jpg/250px-Avon_Fantasy_Reader_18.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="368" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Avon_Fantasy_Reader_18.jpg/400px-Avon_Fantasy_Reader_18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Avon_Fantasy_Reader_18.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="589" /></a><figcaption>The cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Avon_Fantasy_Reader" class="mw-redirect" title="Avon Fantasy Reader">Avon Fantasy Reader</a></i> magazine (number 18)</figcaption></figure>
<p>According to 2013 statistics from the fantasy publisher <a href="/wiki/Tor_Books" title="Tor Books">Tor Books</a>, men outnumber women by 67% to 33% among writers of historical, epic or high fantasy. By contrast, among writers of urban fantasy or paranormal romance, 57% are women and 43% are men.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Analysis">Analysis</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Analysis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Fantasy is studied in a number of disciplines including <a href="/wiki/English_studies" title="English studies">English</a> and other language studies, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_studies" title="Cultural studies">cultural studies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comparative_literature" title="Comparative literature">comparative literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_studies" title="Medieval studies">medieval studies</a>. Some works draw political, historical, and literary connections between medievalism and popular culture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The French literary theorist <a href="/wiki/Tzvetan_Todorov" title="Tzvetan Todorov">Tzvetan Todorov</a> argues that the fantastic is a <a href="/wiki/Liminality" title="Liminality">liminal space</a>, characterized by the intrusion of supernatural elements into the realistic framework of a story, accompanied by uncertainty about their existence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, this precise definition is not predominant in English critical literature; the French term <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastique" title="Fantastique">fantastique</a></i> is used to differentiate the French concept from the broader English term <i>fantastic</i>, a synonym of fantasy. Todorov's restrictive definition and differences in national critical traditions have led to controversies such as the one initiated by <a href="/wiki/Stanislaw_Lem" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanislaw Lem">Stanislaw Lem</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Rosemary Jackson builds on and also challenges Todorov's definition of the fantastic in her book <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fantasy:_The_Literature_of_Subversion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fantasy: The Literature of Subversion (page does not exist)">Fantasy: The Literature of Subversion</a></i> (1981). Jackson rejects the notion of the genre as a vessel for wish fulfillment that transcends human reality in worlds presented as superior to our own; instead she posits that the genre is inseparable from real life, particularly the social and cultural contexts in which each work of the genre is produced. She writes that the "unreal" elements of fantastic literature are created only in direct contrast to the boundaries set by its time period's "cultural order"; these elements act to illuminate the unseen limitations of these boundaries, by undoing and recompiling the structures that define society into something "strange" and "apparently new". In subverting these societal norms, Jackson claims, the fantastic represents an unspoken desire for greater societal change. Jackson criticizes Todorov's theory as being too limited in scope, examining only the literary function of the fantastic; she expands his structuralist theory to fit a more cultural study of the genre—which she proposes is not actually a genre, but a mode that draws upon literary elements of both realistic and supernatural fiction, to create an air of uncertainty in fantastic narratives as described by Todorov. Jackson also introduces the idea of reading the fantastic through a psychoanalytical lens, referring primarily to Freud's theory of the unconscious, which she believes is integral to understanding the fantastic's connection to the human psyche.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are other ways to view the fantastic, and often these different perspectives come from different social climates. In their introduction to <i>The Female Fantastic: Gender and the Supernatural in the 1890s and 1920s</i>, Lizzie Harris McCormick, Jennifer Mitchell, and Rebecca Soares describe how the social climate in the 1890s and 1920s allowed for a new era of fantastic literature to develop. Women were exploring new freedoms and becoming more equal in society. Public fears about such women in society, together with women's expanded roles, allowed them to create a new style of <i>fuzzy</i> supernatural texts. The fantastic sits on the boundary between the supernatural and the mundane; this is analogous to how many women no longer respected a boundary of inequality that had been created for them. At the time, women's roles in society were uncertain; this is similar to how the rules of the fantastic genre are rarely straightforward. This climate allowed for a genre resembling the social structure to emerge, in which the fantastic is never purely supernatural, nor can the supernatural be entirely ruled out. (Similarly, women were not fully equal yet, nor were they completely oppressed.) The <i>female fantastic</i> seeks to reinforce the idea that nothing is certain in the fantastic genre nor in the gender roles of the 1920s. Many women began to blur the lines between genders, removing the binary aspect of gender and allowing for multiple interpretations. In a new way, women began to possess more masculine or <i><a href="/wiki/Queer" title="Queer">queer</a></i> qualities without encountering as much resistance. The fantastic genre reflects these new ideas by breaking analogous boundaries in the supernatural realm, so that readers never fully know whether the story is supernatural.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p>
